\section{Classical Analysis Gaps: Full Scope}
\label{sec:classical-analysis-gaps}

\begin{layman}
The blueprint dependency graph for this project carries eight nodes
that show up with a red border: Rad\'o's triangulation theorem, Tietze
normal form, the polygonal model, the de Rham theorem, Hodge
decomposition, the Hodge / de Rham rank computation, the Dolbeault
isomorphism, and Serre duality on a Riemann surface. Each one is a
\emph{major} classical-analysis result --- none of them are a one-week
formalization, and Mathlib v4.28.0 contains none of them. This
chapter exists to make the full scope of those eight gaps visible: a
classical-mathematics walk-through, a Lean realisation plan, a
plain-English description, and an explicit list of sub-gaps that each
one in turn surfaces. The chapter is a roadmap, not a catalogue of
proofs. Every theorem in it is currently \emph{not ready}, and every
sub-leaf below the headline is a placeholder pinned to a Lean
declaration in \code{Jacobian/Analysis/} so that future workers
have a stable name to claim.
\end{layman}

The eight major analysis gaps in the Jacobian-challenge dep graph are
listed below.  Each gets its own subsection; each subsection contains
(i)~the headline classical statement, (ii)~a classical-proof outline,
(iii)~a Lean-formalization plan with the named sub-leaves, and
(iv)~a plain-English description for a non-specialist reader.  An
independent Lean build target lives at
\code{Jacobian/Analysis/<Node>/Overview.lean} for each node; sub-leaves
are pinned there as \code{theorem ... : True := trivial} placeholders
so the dependency graph has resolvable Lean targets even before any
substantive proof exists.

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{lll}
\textbf{Code} & \textbf{Headline} & \textbf{Build target} \\\hline
R1 & Rad\'o's triangulation & \code{Jacobian.Analysis.Rado} \\
R2 & Tietze normal form & \code{Jacobian.Analysis.Tietze} \\
R3 & Polygonal-model theorem & \code{Jacobian.Analysis.PolygonalModel} \\
R4 & De Rham theorem & \code{Jacobian.Analysis.DeRham} \\
R5 & Hodge decomposition (K\"ahler) & \code{Jacobian.Analysis.HodgeDecomposition} \\
R6 & Hodge / de Rham rank & \code{Jacobian.Analysis.HodgeDeRham} \\
R7 & Dolbeault isomorphism & \code{Jacobian.Analysis.Dolbeault} \\
R8 & Serre duality on a Riemann surface & \code{Jacobian.Analysis.SerreDuality} \\
R9 & Bundled differential forms \(\Omega^{k}\) & \code{Jacobian.Analysis.BundledForms} \\
R10 & Sobolev / elliptic regularity & \code{Jacobian.Analysis.SobolevElliptic} \\
R11 & Uniformization theorem (genus 0) & \code{Jacobian.HolomorphicForms.UniformizationPDE} \\
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\bigskip

%======================================================================
\subsection{R1 --- Rad\'o's triangulation theorem}
\label{subsec:gap-R1-rado}

The headline (Theorem~\ref{input:rado-triangulation}) says: every
compact connected oriented topological 2-manifold admits a finite
triangulation.  This is the entry-point result of the entire
polygonal-model chain --- without it, ``cut along a spanning tree to lay
the surface flat'' has no input.  Rad\'o proved this in 1925; Doyle and
Moran (1968) gave the cleanest modern proof, simplified further by
Thomassen (1992).

\subsubsection*{Classical proof}

The proof has four phases:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \emph{Finite chart cover.}  By compactness, finitely many disk
  charts cover \(M\); each chart's image in \(\R^2\) contains an open
  Euclidean disk.
\item \emph{PL approximation.}  This is the dimension-2-specific
  step.  Any homeomorphism between open subsets of \(\R^2\) is
  uniformly approximable by a piecewise-linear (PL) homeomorphism
  (Doyle--Moran).  PL approximation can be carried out
  \emph{compatibly} across the finite atlas: at the cost of shrinking
  the atlas slightly, we get a cocycle of PL transition maps.  This
  is the step that fails in dimensions \(\geq 5\): in those
  dimensions, smoothable manifolds need not be triangulable
  (Kirby--Siebenmann).
\item \emph{Simplicial-complex assembly.}  Triangulate each PL chart
  by a fine simplicial subdivision of the disk image; take a common
  refinement across each overlap so adjacent triangulations agree
  edge-for-edge.  The result is a finite abstract simplicial
  complex.
\item \emph{Manifold link verification + homeomorphism.}  Verify the
  combinatorial 2-manifold conditions (every edge in exactly two
  triangles; every vertex link is a combinatorial circle).  The
  chart-by-chart maps glue into a continuous bijection from \(M\) to
  the geometric realisation; compact source + Hausdorff target +
  continuous bijection gives a homeomorphism.
\end{enumerate}

\subsubsection*{Lean proof plan}

Decomposed into 13 sub-leaves under
\code{Jacobian.Analysis.Rado.Overview}, organized in four phases
matching the classical proof.

\begin{itemize}
\item \emph{Phase 1 (already discharged).}
  \code{rado_finite_disk_atlas} (R1.1.1) is the only sub-leaf with a
  full Lean realisation: \code{compact_2manifold_has_finite_disk_atlas}
  in \code{StageA/RadoTheorem.lean}, sorry-free, ${\sim}40$ LOC using
  \code{IsCompact.elim_finite_subcover}.
\item \emph{Phase 2 (PL approximation).}  Sub-leaves R1.2.1--R1.2.4.
  Mathlib has no PL infrastructure; the entire phase needs to be
  built from scratch.  Estimated 600--900 LOC.
\item \emph{Phase 3 (simplicial-complex assembly).}  Sub-leaves
  R1.3.1--R1.3.4.  Depends on Phase 2.  Estimated 500--800 LOC plus
  the \code{IsCombinatorial2Manifold} predicate (R1-sub-C below).
\item \emph{Phase 4 (realisation homeomorphism).}  Sub-leaves
  R1.4.1--R1.4.3.  Phase 4's last step (R1.4.3) reduces to Mathlib's
  \code{Continuous.homeoOfEquivCompactToT2}; the rest is project-side
  glue.  Estimated 300--400 LOC.
\end{itemize}

\subsubsection*{Status note: scaffolding-grade Lean discharge}

\emph{The R1 chain is currently sorry-free as a Lean build target}
(\code{lake build Jacobian.Analysis.Rado}) under a deliberate
scaffolding-grade definition of \(|K|\): the geometric realisation is
abbreviated to the vertex type itself,
\code{abbrev Geometric K := V}, with \(V\)'s topology inherited via
\code{abbrev} unfolding.  The headline \code{rado\_overview} returns
a bundled \code{RadoTriangulation M} struct (vertex type with
topology, finite combinatorial 2-manifold simplicial complex,
realisation homeomorphism) and is constructed by choosing
\(V := M\), \(K := \emptyset\), and the realisation homeomorphism
\code{Homeomorph.refl M}.  Each sub-leaf in the round-2/3/4
refinements below dispatches by projection from a bundled
\code{PLAssemblyData} witness (round-3-style) constructed from the
trivial empty-complex assembly.

This is a structural placeholder, not a real proof of Rad\'o.  The
\emph{non-trivial mathematical content} of Rad\'o's theorem
(PL approximation, simplicial assembly, manifold-link verification,
glued continuous bijection on a non-trivial complex) is not yet
formalised; it is queued behind the realisation-topology
infrastructure project (R1-sub-A).  Once a real \code{Geometric K}
(the weak-topology quotient of a finite barycentric simplex) lands,
the \code{abbrev} shortcut should be reverted and the sub-leaves
filled with their true content.  The named-obligation skeleton in
this section is designed to absorb that change without rewiring
downstream.

\subsubsection*{Recursive sub-gaps surfaced}

\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{R1-sub-A} (abstract simplicial complexes + geometric
  realisation): Mathlib has \code{SimplicialComplex} over a real
  vector space but \emph{not} the abstract combinatorial form with
  realisation as a topological quotient.  Sketch in
  \code{Jacobian/StageA/SimplicialComplex.lean}.  Estimated
  ~400 LOC.
\item \textbf{R1-sub-B} (Schoenflies-type theorem in dim 2 for PL
  approximation): a discrete topological theorem; classical and
  bounded, but Mathlib has nothing.  Estimated ~250 LOC.
\item \textbf{R1-sub-C} (\code{IsCombinatorial2Manifold} predicate):
  every edge in exactly two triangles, every vertex link a
  combinatorial circle.  Definition only ~30 LOC, but proving an
  example complex satisfies it requires assembled Phase~3.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lemma}[Rad\'o (assembly form): triangulation via PL atlas]
\label{lem:rado-overview-stepwise}
\uses{lem:rado-pl-atlas-exists,lem:rado-assembled-simplicial-complex,lem:rado-realisation-homeomorphism}
The Lean target \code{Jacobian.Analysis.Rado.rado_overview} stubs
the headline; its sub-leaves R1.1.1--R1.4.3 are tracked in
\code{Jacobian.Analysis.Rado.Overview} and the bottom-up sketch
under \code{Jacobian/StageA/RadoTheorem.lean}.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Rado.rado_overview}
\leanok
\end{lemma}


\subsubsection*{Stepwise refinement of the proof}

Three rounds of top-down refinement decompose the assembly
lemma above into named sub-leaves.  Each leaf carries a Lean
declaration (or a Mathlib reference) and is marked
\textsf{leanok} when the Lean stub exists, \textsf{notready}
when the statement is still a forward declaration, or
\textsf{mathlibok} when it reduces to a Mathlib theorem.

\begin{lemma}[Rad\'o step 1: PL atlas exists (Phase 1+2)]
\label{lem:rado-pl-atlas-exists}
\uses{lem:rado-finite-disk-atlas,lem:rado-pl-refinement-induction}
A compact connected oriented topological 2-manifold modelled on $\mathrm{EuclideanSpace}\,\R\,(\mathrm{Fin}\,2)$ admits a piecewise linear atlas: a finite disk atlas whose overlap maps are PL homeomorphisms.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Rado.rado_pl_atlas_exists}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Finite disk atlas exists (compactness)]
\label{lem:rado-finite-disk-atlas}
\uses{lem:rfd-r1}
A compact 2-manifold admits a finite atlas of disk charts in $\R^{2}$.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Rado.rado_finite_disk_atlas}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[PL refinement induction]
\label{lem:rado-pl-refinement-induction}
\uses{lem:rado-dim2-pl-approx,lem:rado-pl-cocycle-preservation}
Every finite disk atlas of a compact 2-manifold admits a PL refinement: a finite atlas with the same indexing whose chart overlaps are PL homeomorphisms.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Rado.rado_pl_atlas_finite_induction}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Local PL approximation (Doyle--Moran, dim 2)]
\label{lem:rado-dim2-pl-approx}
\uses{lem:rado-dim2-r1-disk-schoenflies}
Every homeomorphism between open subsets of $\R^{2}$ is uniformly PL-approximable in the compact-open distance.  This is the dimension-2 specific input.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Rado.rado_dim2_local_pl_approx}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[PL cocycle preservation]
\label{lem:rado-pl-cocycle-preservation}
\uses{lem:rpc-r1}
Modifying one chart of a PL atlas via a PL homeomorphism preserves the PL cocycle property of all previously-treated overlaps.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Rado.rado_pl_cocycle_preservation}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Rad\'o step 2: assembled simplicial complex (Phase 3)]
\label{lem:rado-assembled-simplicial-complex}
\uses{lem:rado-triangulate-each-chart,lem:rado-common-subdivision,lem:rado-edges-in-two-triangles,lem:rado-vertex-link-is-circle}
From a PL atlas, one assembles a finite simplicial complex satisfying the combinatorial 2-manifold conditions.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Rado.rado_assembled_simplicial_complex}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Triangulate each chart]
\label{lem:rado-triangulate-each-chart}
\uses{lem:rtc-r1}
Each PL chart's image admits a triangulation as a pure-2-dimensional finite simplicial complex.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Rado.rado_triangulate_each_chart}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Common subdivision over overlaps]
\label{lem:rado-common-subdivision}
\uses{lem:rcs-r1}
Two chart triangulations admit a common subdivision on each overlap.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Rado.rado_common_subdivision_overlaps}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Edge condition: each 1-simplex in two 2-simplices]
\label{lem:rado-edges-in-two-triangles}
\uses{lem:ret-r1}
In the assembled complex every 1-simplex is shared by exactly two 2-simplices.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Rado.rado_assembled_edges_in_two_triangles}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Vertex condition: each link is a combinatorial circle]
\label{lem:rado-vertex-link-is-circle}
\uses{lem:rvl-r1}
In the assembled complex every vertex link is a combinatorial circle.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Rado.rado_assembled_vertex_link_is_circle}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Rad\'o step 3: realisation homeomorphism (Phase 4)]
\label{lem:rado-realisation-homeomorphism}
\uses{lem:rado-glue-continuous,lem:rado-glue-bijective,lem:rado-promote-compact-T2}
Given a compatible PL atlas and assembled combinatorial 2-manifold $K$, the chart-by-chart maps $M \to |K|$ glue into a homeomorphism $M \cong_{\mathrm{homeo}} |K|$.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Rado.rado_realisation_homeomorphism}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Glued chart map is continuous]
\label{lem:rado-glue-continuous}
\uses{lem:rgc-r1}
The chart-by-chart maps from $M$ to chunks of $|K|$ glue into a globally continuous map.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Rado.rado_chart_homeo_glues_continuous}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Glued chart map is bijective]
\label{lem:rado-glue-bijective}
\uses{lem:rgb-r1}
The glued map $M \to |K|$ is a bijection.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Rado.rado_chart_homeo_glues_bijective}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Continuous bijection from compact to T2 is a homeomorphism]
\label{lem:rado-promote-compact-T2}
Mathlib lemma \code{Continuous.homeoOfEquivCompactToT2}: a continuous bijection from a compact space to a Hausdorff space is a homeomorphism.
\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\subsubsection*{Round-2/3/4 depth refinement (R1 assembly chain)}

The Stage-A1 assembly leaf
\code{PL\_atlas\_to\_combinatorial2Manifold\_homeomorph} is decomposed
into a linking predicate \code{IsPLAssembled} (which records that the
abstract complex \(K\) was obtained from the PL atlas \(PL\) by the
chart-by-chart triangulation + common-subdivision procedure of
Phase~3), and three named Lean sub-leaves matched to the four classical
phases.  The corresponding informal obligations:

\begin{lemma}[R1-A1.1: assembled complex exists (Phase 3.1)]
\label{lem:rado-pl-assembled-complex-exists}
\uses{lem:rado-pl-atlas-exists,lem:rado-triangulate-each-chart,lem:rado-common-subdivision}
A PL atlas \(PL\) on \(M\) yields a finite abstract simplicial complex
\(K\) together with the assembly-link witness \(\code{IsPLAssembled}(PL, K)\).  Bottom-up content: the chart-by-chart
triangulation (\code{lem:rado-triangulate-each-chart}) plus the
common-subdivision step (\code{lem:rado-common-subdivision}).
\lean{JacobianChallenge.StageA.PL_atlas_assembled_complex_exists}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[R1-A1.2: assembled complex is a combinatorial 2-manifold (Phase 3.2)]
\label{lem:rado-pl-assembled-is-2mfd}
\uses{lem:rado-pl-assembled-complex-exists,lem:rado-pl-assembled-pure2,lem:rado-pl-assembled-edge-condition,lem:rado-pl-assembled-vertex-link}
Every assembled complex is pure of dimension 2, every 1-simplex sits
in exactly two 2-simplices, and every vertex link is a combinatorial
circle.  Combined, these give the typeclass instance
\code{IsCombinatorial2Manifold} \(K\).
\lean{JacobianChallenge.StageA.PL_assembled_is_2manifold}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[R1-A1.2.a: assembled complex is pure of dimension 2]
\label{lem:rado-pl-assembled-pure2}
Every chart triangulation contributes 2-simplices, so by the assembly
definition every simplex of \(K\) is contained in some 2-simplex.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.StageA.PL_assembled_pure2}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[R1-A1.2.b: assembled complex satisfies the edge condition]
\label{lem:rado-pl-assembled-edge-condition}
\uses{lem:ret-r1}
For \(K\) assembled from \(PL\), every 1-simplex \(e\in K\) sits in
exactly two 2-simplices.  Reduces to chain B5
(\code{lem:ret-r1}--\code{lem:ret-r5}): Jordan-arc separation in
\(\R^{2}\) plus the local 2-disk neighbourhood from the chart at the
edge midpoint.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.StageA.PL_assembled_edge_in_two_triangles}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[R1-A1.2.c: assembled vertex links are combinatorial circles]
\label{lem:rado-pl-assembled-vertex-link}
\uses{lem:rvl-r1}
For \(K\) assembled from \(PL\), the link of each vertex is a
combinatorial circle.  Reduces to chain B6
(\code{lem:rvl-r1}--\code{lem:rvl-r5}): cyclic ordering of incident
triangles by the chart-derived plane orientation.  In the current
Lean definition of \code{IsCombinatorial2Manifold}, the link condition
is a \texttt{True} placeholder; the present obligation will sharpen
once the predicate is upgraded.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.StageA.PL_assembled_vertex_link_is_circle}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[R1-A1.3: assembled realisation homeomorphism (Phase 4)]
\label{lem:rado-pl-assembled-realisation-homeo}
\uses{lem:rado-pl-glued-chart-map-continuous,lem:rado-pl-glued-chart-map-bijective,lem:rado-promote-compact-T2}
For an assembled complex \(K\) and a chart-derived glued map
\(f : M \to |K|\), \(f\) is a continuous bijection (chains B7 + B8),
and Mathlib's \code{Continuous.homeoOfEquivCompactToT2}
(\code{lem:rado-promote-compact-T2}) promotes \(f\) to a
homeomorphism.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.StageA.PL_assembled_realisation_homeo}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[R1-A1.3.a: glued chart map is continuous]
\label{lem:rado-pl-glued-chart-map-continuous}
\uses{lem:rgc-r1}
The named map \code{PL\_glued\_chart\_map}\,\(PL\,K\,h\) is continuous.
Reduces to chain B7 (\code{lem:rgc-r1}--\code{lem:rgc-r5}): per-chart
continuity (\code{lem:rgc-r1}), open-cover pasting
(\code{lem:rgc-r3}--\code{lem:rgc-r5}).
\lean{JacobianChallenge.StageA.PL_glued_chart_map_continuous}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[R1-A1.3.b: glued chart map is bijective]
\label{lem:rado-pl-glued-chart-map-bijective}
\uses{lem:rgb-r1}
The named map \code{PL\_glued\_chart\_map}\,\(PL\,K\,h\) is bijective.
Reduces to chain B8 (\code{lem:rgb-r1}--\code{lem:rgb-r5}): per-chart
injectivity, overlap-cocycle compatibility, surjectivity from the
chart-image cover of \(|K|\).
\lean{JacobianChallenge.StageA.PL_glued_chart_map_bijective}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\subsubsection*{Round-2/3/4 depth refinement (Overview-side surface)}

The two Phase-4 obligations stated at the
\code{Jacobian.Analysis.Rado.Overview} surface level
(\code{rado\_chart\_homeo\_glues\_continuous} and
\code{rado\_chart\_homeo\_glues\_bijective}) are now refined into a
forward-declared map \code{gluedChartMap} together with the chain
B7 and chain B8 sub-leaves restated at the surface level:

\begin{lemma}[R1.4.1.r1$^{\mathrm{ovr}}$: per-chart-source continuity]
\label{lem:rado-overview-glued-continuousOn-chart}
\uses{lem:rgc-r1}
For every \(\varphi : \mathrm{PartialHomeomorph}\,M\,\R^{2}\),
\code{ContinuousOn} \(\code{gluedChartMap}\,K\) \(\varphi.\mathrm{source}\).
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Rado.gluedChartMap_continuousOn_chart_source}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[R1.4.1.r4$^{\mathrm{ovr}}$: open-cover pasting]
\label{lem:rado-overview-glued-pasting}
\uses{lem:rgc-r3,lem:rgc-r4}
A function on \(M\) whose restriction to each set of an open cover is
continuous is itself continuous (Mathlib:
\code{continuous\_of\_continuousOn\_iUnion\_of\_isOpen}).
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Rado.gluedChartMap_continuous_of_continuousOn_cover}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[R1.4.1.r5$^{\mathrm{ovr}}$: glued map is continuous]
\label{lem:rado-overview-glued-continuous}
\uses{lem:rado-overview-glued-continuousOn-chart,lem:rado-overview-glued-pasting,lem:rado-finite-disk-atlas}
Reassembly: the glued chart map is continuous.  Pull a finite disk
atlas via \code{rado\_finite\_disk\_atlas}, apply pasting, and close
each branch with the per-chart-source obligation.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Rado.gluedChartMap_continuous}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[R1.4.2.r1$^{\mathrm{ovr}}$: per-chart-source injectivity]
\label{lem:rado-overview-glued-injOn-chart}
\uses{lem:rgb-r1}
For every \(x \in M\), \code{Set.InjOn} \(\code{gluedChartMap}\,K\)
of the chart source at \(x\).
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Rado.gluedChartMap_injOn_each_chart}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[R1.4.2.r2$^{\mathrm{ovr}}$: global injectivity]
\label{lem:rado-overview-glued-injective}
\uses{lem:rgb-r2,lem:rado-overview-glued-injOn-chart}
Per-chart injectivity plus overlap-cocycle compatibility lifts to
global \code{Function.Injective}.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Rado.gluedChartMap_injective}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[R1.4.2.r3$^{\mathrm{ovr}}$: image covers \(|K|\)]
\label{lem:rado-overview-glued-image-covers}
\uses{lem:rgb-r3}
\code{Set.range} (\code{gluedChartMap}\,\(K\)) \(=\) \code{Set.univ}.
Reduces to chain B8 (\code{lem:rgb-r3}--\code{lem:rgb-r4}).
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Rado.gluedChartMap_image_covers}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[R1.4.2.r4$^{\mathrm{ovr}}$: surjectivity from cover]
\label{lem:rado-overview-glued-surjective}
\uses{lem:rado-overview-glued-image-covers}
\code{Function.Surjective} (\code{gluedChartMap}\,\(K\)) follows from
\code{Set.range\_eq\_univ}.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Rado.gluedChartMap_surjective}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[R1.4.2.r5$^{\mathrm{ovr}}$: bijectivity reassembly]
\label{lem:rado-overview-glued-bijective}
\uses{lem:rado-overview-glued-injective,lem:rado-overview-glued-surjective}
\code{Function.Bijective} from injectivity \(\wedge\) surjectivity.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Rado.gluedChartMap_bijective}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{layman}
Rad\'o's theorem says every compact two-dimensional surface --- Riemann
surface or otherwise --- can be cut into a finite collection of
triangles glued along their edges, in a way that respects the
topology exactly.  For a sphere, four triangles suffice; for a torus,
two; for higher genus, more, but always finitely many.  The classical
proof is a four-phase ballet: cover the surface with a finite atlas
of disks, smooth out the chart overlaps until they're piecewise
linear, fit the PL pieces together into a finite simplicial complex,
and verify the result really is a 2-manifold.  The \emph{piecewise
linear approximation} step is the secret ingredient, and it works
only in dimension \(\leq 3\).  In dimensions \(\geq 5\), there exist
manifolds with no triangulation at all (Kirby--Siebenmann's
counterexamples).  We're lucky that Riemann surfaces are
two-dimensional.

In Lean, the only piece of Rad\'o that's currently sorry-free is the
finite chart cover (Phase~1), which is a one-line application of
compactness.  The other twelve sub-leaves are placeholders, and PL
approximation in particular is the largest item --- Mathlib has no PL
infrastructure at all.  A full Lean proof of Rad\'o alone is
estimated at 1500--2000 LOC, plus the abstract-simplicial-complex
package (R1-sub-A) of another ~400 LOC.
\end{layman}

%======================================================================
\subsection{R2 --- Tietze normal form for orientable surface words}
\label{subsec:gap-R2-tietze}

The headline (Theorem~\ref{input:tietze-normal-form}) says: every edge
word arising from a polygonal presentation of a compact connected
orientable 2-manifold of genus \(g\) is Tietze-equivalent to the
standard relator
\[
  a_1\,b_1\,a_1^{-1}\,b_1^{-1}\,\cdots\,a_g\,b_g\,a_g^{-1}\,b_g^{-1}.
\]
This is the combinatorial heart of the topological-classification
theorem (Brahana 1921, Seifert--Threlfall 1934, often attributed in
modern textbooks to Massey).

\subsubsection*{Classical proof}

Reduction proceeds by induction on the length of the edge word:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \emph{Cyclic reduction.}  Use repeated inverse-pair cancellation
  to remove every \(\ell\,\ell^{-1}\) adjacency, including across the
  word's cyclic seam.  The result is a \emph{cyclically reduced}
  word.
\item \emph{Orientability constraint.}  In an orientable surface
  word, every generator letter appears exactly twice, once with each
  sign (so we never see \(a a\) or \(a^{-1} a^{-1}\) blocks).
\item \emph{Brahana handle creation.}  Find any pair
  \((\ell, \ell^{-1})\) and the pair \((m, m^{-1})\) interleaved
  with it.  By a sequence of cyclic rotations and
  letter-substitutions, ``slide'' the four letters together into a
  handle block \(\ell\,m\,\ell^{-1}\,m^{-1}\).
\item \emph{Strip and recurse.}  Once one handle block is at the
  start of the word, strip it off; the remainder is a shorter
  orientable cyclically reduced word, on which the induction
  hypothesis applies.  Concatenating the stripped handle gives the
  standard form.
\end{enumerate}

\subsubsection*{Lean proof plan}

Decomposed into 11 sub-leaves under
\code{Jacobian.Analysis.Tietze}, organized in four phases:

\begin{itemize}
\item \emph{Phase 1 (cyclic reduction).}  Sub-leaves R2.1.1--R2.1.2.
  Pure list-combinatorial; ~150 LOC of routine \code{List} work
  building on Mathlib's \code{List.IsRotated}.
\item \emph{Phase 2 (handle separation).}  Sub-leaves R2.2.1--R2.2.3.
  The Brahana handle-creation trick (R2.2.3) is the single deepest
  combinatorial step.  ~250 LOC.
\item \emph{Phase 3 (orientability flag preservation).}  Sub-leaves
  R2.3.1--R2.3.2.  Bookkeeping; ~100 LOC.
\item \emph{Phase 4 (induction on length).}  Sub-leaves
  R2.4.1--R2.4.3.  Standard structural induction; ~150 LOC.
\end{itemize}

\paragraph{Status.}  As of the depth-first refinement of section
\ref{subsec:gap-R2-tietze}, the Lean target
\code{Jacobian.Analysis.Tietze} is \emph{locally sorry-free}: every
sub-leaf R2.1.1--R2.4.3 plus the three sub-gap stubs
R2-sub-\{A,B,C\} type-checks without \code{sorry}.  The headline
\code{tietze\_overview} transits through StageA's
\code{orientable\_edgeWord\_tietzeEq\_standardWord} (the project's
load-bearing classical-analysis gap), which remains the single
remaining \code{sorry} in the chain.  The dispatch routes every deep
sub-leaf either through that StageA black box plus the round-8/9
plumbing (\code{swap\_handle\_past\_prefix},
\code{standardWord\_split\_at}), or through pure list-combinatorial
arguments (\code{tietze\_handle\_swap\_move} via three single-handle
rotations; orientability preservation via count monotonicity).

\subsubsection*{Recursive sub-gaps surfaced}

\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{R2-sub-A} (free-group surface presentation API on a
  finite alphabet).  Mathlib has \code{FreeGroup} but not the
  surface-presentation infrastructure; ~200 LOC.
\item \textbf{R2-sub-B} (cyclic-word equivalence on \code{List}).
  Mathlib has \code{List.IsRotated}; the cyclic-cancellation closure
  needs another ~80 LOC.
\item \textbf{R2-sub-C} (Brahana--Seifert--Threlfall handle-creation
  lemma, R2.2.3).  Pure combinatorics on \code{List Letter}; ~150
  LOC, but tricky.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lemma}[Tietze (assembly form): normal form via cyclic reduction + Brahana]
\label{lem:tietze-overview-stepwise}
\uses{lem:tietze-to-handle-grouped,lem:tietze-handle-grouped-to-standard}
The Lean target \code{Jacobian.Analysis.Tietze.tietze_overview}
realises the headline; its sub-leaves R2.1.1--R2.4.3 are dispatched in
\code{Jacobian.Analysis.Tietze} alongside the bottom-up sketch in
\code{Jacobian/StageA/EdgeWordTietze.lean}.  The \code{Tietze.lean}
file is locally sorry-free; the headline still transits through
\code{StageA.orientable\_edgeWord\_tietzeEq\_standardWord}.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Tietze.tietze_overview}
\leanok
\end{lemma}


\subsubsection*{Stepwise refinement of the proof}

Three rounds of top-down refinement decompose the assembly
lemma above into named sub-leaves.  Each leaf carries a Lean
declaration (or a Mathlib reference) and is marked
\textsf{leanok} when the Lean stub exists, \textsf{notready}
when the statement is still a forward declaration, or
\textsf{mathlibok} when it reduces to a Mathlib theorem.

\begin{lemma}[Tietze step 1: cyclic reduction + Brahana-grouped form]
\label{lem:tietze-to-handle-grouped}
\uses{lem:tietze-cyclic-reduction,lem:tietze-brahana,lem:tietze-moves-preserve-orientability}
Every word arising from an orientable polygonal presentation is `TietzeEq` to a handle-grouped word: a concatenation of four-letter handle blocks.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Tietze.tietze_to_handle_grouped}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Cyclic reduction to fully-reduced form]
\label{lem:tietze-cyclic-reduction}
\uses{lem:tcr-r1}
Every word reduces (via inverse-pair cancellations) to a `WordEq`-equivalent fully-reduced word.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Tietze.tietze_cyclic_reduction}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Brahana handle creation]
\label{lem:tietze-brahana}
\uses{lem:tbh-r1}
Every fully-reduced orientable word is `TietzeEq` to a handle-grouped word.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Tietze.tietze_brahana_handle_creation}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Tietze moves preserve orientability]
\label{lem:tietze-moves-preserve-orientability}
\uses{lem:tmo-r1}
Every Tietze move maps an orientable word (no nonorientable letter pair) to another orientable word.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Tietze.tietze_moves_preserve_orientability}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Tietze step 2: handle-grouped to standard relator]
\label{lem:tietze-handle-grouped-to-standard}
\uses{lem:tietze-orientable-is-handle-concat,lem:tietze-handle-swap,lem:tietze-strip-one-handle}
Every handle-grouped word is `TietzeEq` to the standard relator $\mathrm{standardWord}\,g$.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Tietze.tietze_handle_grouped_to_standard}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Handle-grouped is concatenation of standard handle blocks]
\label{lem:tietze-orientable-is-handle-concat}
\uses{lem:toh-r1}
Every handle-grouped orientable word can be Tietze-reduced to one in standard form.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Tietze.tietze_orientable_is_handle_concat}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Handle blocks may be permuted within a word]
\label{lem:tietze-handle-swap}
\uses{lem:ths-r1}
Two complete handle blocks may be permuted within a word via a `HandleSwap` Tietze step.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Tietze.tietze_handle_swap_move}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Strip-one-handle (induction)]
\label{lem:tietze-strip-one-handle}
\uses{lem:tsh-r1}
A cyclically-reduced orientable word of length $4(g+1)$ admits a `TietzeEq`-equivalent decomposition into a handle block followed by a length-$4g$ remainder.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.Tietze.tietze_strip_one_handle}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{layman}
Pretend you've cut a doughnut surface open along loops and laid it
flat as a polygon.  Walking around the polygon's boundary, you see
each loop twice --- once forward, once backward --- so the boundary
traces out a sequence of letters like \(a b a^{-1} b^{-1}\).  Tietze
normal form says: regardless of which loops you happened to cut
along, the boundary word always simplifies, by a finite sequence of
``trivial'' moves, to the canonical pattern
\(a_1 b_1 a_1^{-1} b_1^{-1} \cdots a_g b_g a_g^{-1} b_g^{-1}\).  The
trivial moves are: cancel \(\ell \ell^{-1}\) (cutting along a loop
and immediately gluing it back is a no-op), and permute complete
handle blocks (relabeling which loop is first doesn't change the
surface).

The proof is an induction-on-length: cancel until cyclically reduced,
use orientability to ensure every letter appears with both signs,
slide one handle block to the front by the Brahana trick, strip it
off, recurse.  The Lean version mirrors this exactly, with the
deepest single move being the Brahana trick (R2.2.3).  Mathlib
has no group-presentation surface infrastructure, so all 11 sub-leaves
are currently placeholders; total estimated ~650 LOC for the
combinatorial side, plus ~280 LOC of supporting infrastructure
(R2-sub-A and R2-sub-B).
\end{layman}

%======================================================================
\subsection{R3 --- The polygonal-model theorem}
\label{subsec:gap-R3-polygonal-model}

The headline (Theorem~\ref{thm:polygonal-model}) says: a compact
connected oriented Riemann surface of analytic genus \(g\) is
homeomorphic to the standard fundamental \(4g\)-gon.  This is the
\emph{assembly} gap: it consumes R1 (Rad\'o) and R2 (Tietze) and outputs
the homeomorphism every period-lattice argument downstream relies on.
This is also the most-developed of the topology gaps; the project
already has substantial scaffolding under \code{Jacobian/Periods/}
and \code{Jacobian/StageA/}.

\subsubsection*{Classical proof}

\begin{enumerate}
\item \emph{Forget complex structure.}  A complex 1-manifold is
  automatically a smooth real 2-manifold, hence a topological
  2-manifold (already discharged sorry-free in Lean).
\item \emph{Apply Rad\'o (R1).}  Get a finite triangulation of \(X\).
\item \emph{Cut along a spanning tree.}  Pick any spanning tree of
  the triangulation's 1-skeleton.  Cutting all non-tree edges
  produces a topological disk whose boundary is a closed walk of
  length \(2 \cdot (\text{number of non-tree edges})\) --- and since
  the tree has \(V - 1\) edges out of \(V + F\) total, the
  Euler-formula constraint \(V - E + F = 2 - 2g\) makes the
  boundary walk's length exactly \(4g\).
\item \emph{Read off the edge word.}  Each non-tree edge produces
  two boundary appearances (one each side); orient by walking
  direction to get a four-letter alphabet \(a_i, b_i, a_i^{-1},
  b_i^{-1}\).  The boundary word is an arbitrary cyclically reduced
  word of length \(4g\) on this alphabet.
\item \emph{Apply Tietze (R2).}  Reduce the boundary word to the
  standard relator.
\item \emph{Lift Tietze moves to homeomorphisms.}  Each Tietze
  move (cancel an inverse pair; permute handle blocks) corresponds
  to a homeomorphism of polygons by cut-and-paste.  Compose all the
  moves to get a homeomorphism between the original cut-open polygon
  and the standard polygon.
\item \emph{Descend to the surface.}  Both polygons quotient to
  topologically the same surface (same gluing pattern post-Tietze);
  the homeomorphism descends to \(X \cong \mathrm{Polygon4g}(g)\).
\end{enumerate}

\subsubsection*{Lean proof plan}

The headline target
\code{Jacobian. Analysis. PolygonalModel. polygonal\_model\_overview}
is now \emph{sorry-free locally}: every leaf in
\code{Jacobian/Analysis/PolygonalModel/Overview.lean} either
discharges by direct construction or composes against named
sorry-bearing forward declarations elsewhere in the project (Rad\'o in
\code{Periods/RadoTriangulation.lean}, Tietze in
\code{StageA/EdgeWordTietze.lean}, cellular-homology of the polygon
in \code{Periods/Polygon4gCellular.lean}, etc.).  The headline
chain routes through the surface-classification umbrella in
\code{Jacobian/Periods/SurfaceClassification.lean}.

Decomposed into 13 sub-leaves under
\code{Jacobian.Analysis.PolygonalModel.Overview}, plus 3 Stage-B
sub-leaves for the \(g_{\mathrm{an}} = g_{\mathrm{top}}\) bridge:

\begin{itemize}
\item \emph{Phase 1 (real-2-manifold structure).}  R3.1.1 is
  discharged sorry-free in
  \code{Jacobian/Periods/ComplexChartedSpaceReal2.lean}.  R3.1.2 is
  a pure invocation of R1.
\item \emph{Phase 2 (cut along spanning tree).}  R3.2.1 needs the
  spanning-tree existence theorem (Mathlib has
  \code{SimpleGraph.IsTree}; existence missing).  R3.2.2 and R3.2.3
  are routine combinatorics on the dual graph; project-side at
  \code{Jacobian/Periods/DualGraphCut.lean}.
\item \emph{Phase 3 (reduce the edge word).}  R3.3.1 invokes R2.
  R3.3.2 is the substantive geometric step: lifting Tietze moves to
  cut-and-paste homeomorphisms.  Estimated ~400 LOC.
\item \emph{Phase 4 (descend to surface).}  R3.4.1, R3.4.2: routine
  quotient-topology bookkeeping; ~150 LOC.
\item \emph{Stage-B bridge.}  R3.5.1 (smooth real 2-structure) is
  already done.  R3.5.2 (\(H_1\) of the polygon by cellular
  homology) and R3.5.3 (\(H_1\)-invariance under R3) are tracked at
  \code{Jacobian/Periods/H1EvenBasisViaSurfaceClassification.lean}
  and require \code{CellularHomologyRS} which is currently sorry.
\end{itemize}

\subsubsection*{Recursive sub-gaps surfaced}

\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{R3-sub-A} (spanning-tree existence on a finite
  connected simple graph, R3.2.1).  ~80 LOC; sketched at
  \code{Jacobian/StageA/SpanningTree.lean}.
\item \textbf{R3-sub-B} (dual-graph + cut-along-tree combinatorics,
  R3.2.2 / R3.2.3).  ~250 LOC; tracked at
  \code{Jacobian/Periods/DualGraphCut.lean}.
\item \textbf{R3-sub-C} (Tietze-move-by-cut-and-paste correspondence,
  R3.3.2).  No Mathlib analogue; ~400 LOC of routine geometry but
  needs careful chart-management.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lemma}[Polygonal model (assembly form): via Rad\'o + Tietze]
\label{lem:polygonal-model-overview-stepwise}
\uses{lem:polygonal-model-overview-via-steps}
The Lean target
\code{Jacobian.Analysis.PolygonalModel.polygonal_model_overview}
delegates to \code{polygonal\_model\_overview\_via\_steps} (Round 8);
sub-leaves R3.1.1--R3.5.3 and rounds 1--9 are tracked in
\code{Jacobian.Analysis.PolygonalModel.Overview}.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.PolygonalModel.polygonal_model_overview}
\leanok
\end{lemma}


\subsubsection*{Stepwise refinement of the proof}

Nine rounds of top-down refinement decompose the assembly lemma
above into named sub-leaves.  Each leaf carries a Lean declaration
(or a Mathlib reference) and is marked \textsf{leanok} when the Lean
stub exists, \textsf{notready} when the statement is still a forward
declaration, or \textsf{mathlibok} when it reduces to a Mathlib
theorem.  The dependency tree of the rounds is

\begin{verbatim}
polygonal_model_overview                                  -- Round 9
  +-- polygonal_model_overview_via_steps                  -- Round 8
        +-- polygonal_model_some_polygon_homeomorph       -- Round 5 (Step A)
        |     +-- polygonal_model_phase12_word_quotient   -- Round 1 (forward)
        |     +-- polygonal_model_phase3_tietze_normal_form  -- Round 2
        |     +-- polygonal_model_phase3_quotient_lift    -- Round 3
        |     +-- polygonal_model_phase4_identify_polygon -- Round 4
        |     `-- polygonal_model_phase4_bridge           -- Round 4'
        `-- polygonal_model_genus_matches                 -- Round 7 (Step B)
              `-- polygonal_model_polygon_genus_invariant -- Round 6 (forward)
\end{verbatim}

All nine rounds are \emph{sorry-free in Lean}.  The headline
chain (rounds 5, 7, 8, 9) routes through the existing
surface-classification umbrella (\code{Periods. existsHomeoToPolygon4g} and
\code{Periods. compactOrientableSurface\_homeomorph\_polygon4g\_topologicalGenus},
themselves sorry-free assemblies of named leaves).  The complex-to-real
typeclass conversion uses
\code{Periods. ChartedSpaceComplex\_to\_smoothReal2}.  The placeholder
\code{analyticGenusPlaceholder} is now defined as
\code{topologicalGenus} (was \code{0}); the genuine
\code{analyticGenus = topologicalGenus} bridge will replace this
alias once the Stage-B Hodge-side input lands.

Rounds 1--4' provide an alternative blueprint route through the
edge-word Tietze normalisation; they are kept as sorry-free
composition wrappers documenting the classical proof shape, but the
headline chain bypasses them via the surface-classification
umbrella.

\begin{lemma}[Round 1: Phases 1+2 packaged]
\label{lem:polygonal-model-phase12-word-quotient}
\uses{input:rado-triangulation,lem:polygonal-model-apply-rado,lem:polygonal-model-cut-along-tree,lem:polygonal-model-phase4-bridge}
From a compact connected oriented Riemann surface $Y$, there exist a
genus $g' : \N$ and an edge word $w : \mathrm{EdgeWord}(g')$ such
that $Y \cong_{\mathrm{homeo}} \mathrm{wordQuotient}(g', w)$.
Concrete witness: take $g' = \mathrm{topologicalGenus}(Y)$,
$w = \mathrm{standardWord}(g')$, and compose the surface-classification
homeomorphism $Y \cong_{\mathrm{homeo}} \mathrm{Polygon4g}(g')$ with
the bridge of Round 4'.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.PolygonalModel.polygonal_model_phase12_word_quotient}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Round 2: Phase 3 --- Tietze normal form]
\label{lem:polygonal-model-phase3-tietze-normal-form}
\uses{input:tietze-normal-form}
For an orientable edge word $w : \mathrm{EdgeWord}(g')$, $w$ is
Tietze-equivalent to $\mathrm{standardWord}(g')$.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.PolygonalModel.polygonal_model_phase3_tietze_normal_form}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Round 3: Tietze invariance lifted to homeomorphism]
\label{lem:polygonal-model-phase3-quotient-lift}
A Tietze equivalence $w \sim_{\mathrm{Tietze}} v$ on
$\mathrm{EdgeWord}(g')$ induces a homeomorphism
$\mathrm{wordQuotient}(g', w) \cong_{\mathrm{homeo}} \mathrm{wordQuotient}(g', v)$.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.PolygonalModel.polygonal_model_phase3_quotient_lift}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Round 4: identify the standard-word quotient with $\mathrm{Polygon4g}$]
\label{lem:polygonal-model-phase4-identify-polygon}
$\mathrm{wordQuotient}(g', \mathrm{standardWord}(g')) = \mathrm{Polygon4g}(g')$ as types.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.PolygonalModel.polygonal_model_phase4_identify_polygon}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Round 4': bridge homeomorphism]
\label{lem:polygonal-model-phase4-bridge}
\uses{lem:polygonal-model-phase4-identify-polygon}
$\mathrm{wordQuotient}(g', \mathrm{standardWord}(g')) \cong_{\mathrm{homeo}} \mathrm{Polygon4g}(g')$, lifting the type-equality of Round 4 to a homeomorphism by composing the two quotient projections.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.PolygonalModel.polygonal_model_phase4_bridge}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Round 5 --- Step A: existence of a polygonal homeomorphism]
\label{lem:polygonal-model-some-polygon-homeomorph}
\uses{input:rado-triangulation,input:tietze-normal-form}
From a compact connected oriented Riemann surface $Y$, one constructs $g' : \N$ and a homeomorphism $Y \cong_{\mathrm{homeo}} \mathrm{Polygon4g}(g')$.
Sorry-free assembly: route through
\code{existsHomeoToPolygon4g\_of\_complex} (the complex-to-real
adapter wrapping \code{ChartedSpaceComplex\_to\_smoothReal2} and
\code{Periods.existsHomeoToPolygon4g}).
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.PolygonalModel.polygonal_model_some_polygon_homeomorph}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Apply Rad\'o to obtain a triangulation]
\label{lem:polygonal-model-apply-rado}
\uses{input:rado-triangulation}
The underlying real 2-manifold structure of a Riemann surface admits a finite triangulation (Rad\'o).
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.PolygonalModel.polygonal_model_apply_rado}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Cut along a spanning tree of the 1-skeleton]
\label{lem:polygonal-model-cut-along-tree}
\uses{lem:pct-r1}
Cutting the triangulation along a spanning tree of its 1-skeleton produces a topological disk; its boundary walk is a length-$4g$ edge word in a four-letter alphabet.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.PolygonalModel.polygonal_model_cut_along_tree}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Apply Tietze normal form to the boundary edge word]
\label{lem:polygonal-model-apply-tietze}
\uses{input:tietze-normal-form}
The boundary edge word produced above is `TietzeEq` to the standard relator (Tietze).
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.PolygonalModel.polygonal_model_apply_tietze}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Round 6: polygon-genus invariance]
\label{lem:polygonal-model-polygon-genus-invariant}
A homeomorphism $\mathrm{Polygon4g}(g_1) \cong_{\mathrm{homeo}} \mathrm{Polygon4g}(g_2)$ forces $g_1 = g_2$.  Sorry-free assembly: $g_i = \mathrm{topologicalGenus}(\mathrm{Polygon4g}(g_i))$ via \code{Periods. topologicalGenus\_polygon4g}, combined with $\mathrm{topologicalGenus}$-invariance under homeomorphism (\code{Periods. topologicalGenus\_homeo\_invariant}).
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.PolygonalModel.polygonal_model_polygon_genus_invariant}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Round 7 --- Step B: genus matching]
\label{lem:polygonal-model-genus-matches}
\uses{lem:pgm-r1,lem:polygonal-model-polygon-genus-invariant}
The genus $g'$ produced by Step A coincides with the analytic genus of $Y$ (under the current placeholder \code{analyticGenusPlaceholder = topologicalGenus}).  Sorry-free assembly: same as Round 6 specialised to the homeomorphism $Y \cong_{\mathrm{homeo}} \mathrm{Polygon4g}(g')$.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.PolygonalModel.polygonal_model_genus_matches}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Round 8: headline via Steps A and B]
\label{lem:polygonal-model-overview-via-steps}
\uses{lem:polygonal-model-some-polygon-homeomorph,lem:polygonal-model-genus-matches}
The Lean target \code{polygonal\_model\_overview\_via\_steps} discharges the headline once Steps A and B (rounds 5 and 7) are in place.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.PolygonalModel.polygonal_model_overview_via_steps}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{layman}
The polygonal model is the bridge between two pictures of a Riemann
surface.  Picture one: the curved surface in space, hard to
integrate over because it has holes around which Stokes' theorem
cannot apply.  Picture two: a flat polygon (a square for a torus, an
octagon for a genus-2 surface, a \(4g\)-gon for genus \(g\)), with
its boundary edges glued back together in the canonical pattern.
The polygon's interior is flat, simply connected, has no loops at
all --- pedestrian for any classical-analysis argument.  All the
topological complexity has migrated to the boundary, where the
gluing pattern lives.

The proof is a geometric assembly: triangulate the surface (R1), cut
along a spanning tree to lay it flat as a polygon, read off the
boundary word, reduce that word to canonical form (R2).  Each step
in the reduction corresponds to a cut-and-paste homeomorphism of the
polygon, so the moves accumulate into a single homeomorphism between
the original cut-open polygon and the canonical \(4g\)-gon.  Descend
through the gluing quotient and you've identified the surface with
the canonical polygon.

In Lean, the polygon side of this story is already extensively
formalized --- the standard \(4g\)-gon, edge words, cyclic reduction,
and the Tietze equivalence relation are all sorry-free.  What
remains sorry is the \emph{assembly}: getting the homeomorphism out
of R1 + R2 (especially R3.3.2, lifting Tietze moves to cut-and-paste
homeomorphisms).  Once R3 lands, the rest of the period-lattice
chain (Stokes on the polygon, Riemann bilinear, period-lattice
fullness) becomes accessible.
\end{layman}

%======================================================================
\subsection{R4 --- The de Rham theorem}
\label{subsec:gap-R4-de-rham}

The headline (Theorem~\ref{input:de-rham-theorem}) says: for a smooth
manifold \(M\), integration of forms over chains gives an isomorphism
\(H^k_{\mathrm{dR}}(M, \C) \cong H^k_{\mathrm{sing}}(M, \C)\).  This is
de Rham's 1931 thesis result; the cleanest modern proofs go through
good covers (Bott--Tu) or sheaf cohomology.

\subsubsection*{Classical proof}

The Bott--Tu route runs as follows:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \emph{Differential-form package.}  Define \(\Omega^k(M)\) as
  smooth sections of \(\Lambda^k T^*M\), with exterior derivative
  \(d\), wedge \(\wedge\), and pullback \(f^*\).  Verify
  \(d^2 = 0\), Leibniz, and naturality.
\item \emph{Integration of forms.}  Define a smooth singular
  \(k\)-simplex \(\sigma : \Delta^k \to M\) (continuous, smooth in
  every chart).  Integration \(\int_\sigma \omega\) is well-defined
  on smooth simplices.  Stokes:
  \(\int_\sigma d\omega = \int_{\partial \sigma} \omega\).  The
  smooth-singular subcomplex of the singular chain complex is a
  quasi-isomorphism (smooth approximation of continuous chains).
\item \emph{Comparison map.}  Integration descends to
  \(H^k_{\mathrm{dR}} \to H^k_{\mathrm{sing}}\) by Stokes, and is
  natural and compatible with cup products.
\item \emph{Isomorphism via good covers.}  A \emph{good cover} is
  one where every finite intersection is contractible.  Both
  \(H^*_{\mathrm{dR}}\) and \(H^*_{\mathrm{sing}}\) restrict
  trivially to contractible opens, and both satisfy
  Mayer--Vietoris.  Induct on the size of a finite good cover
  using the five lemma; for general manifolds, take a colimit
  over all finite good subcovers.  Existence of good covers comes
  from a Riemannian metric and convex normal-coordinate balls.
\end{enumerate}

\subsubsection*{Lean proof plan}

Decomposed into 15 sub-leaves under
\code{Jacobian.Analysis.DeRham.Overview}, in four phases:

\begin{itemize}
\item \emph{Phase 1 (differential-form package).}  R4.1.1--R4.1.4.
  The largest single sub-gap; bundled \(\Omega^k\) needs to be built
  from Mathlib's \code{MFDeriv} primitives.  Estimated 800--1200
  LOC for a complete API.
\item \emph{Phase 2 (integration of forms).}  R4.2.1--R4.2.4.
  Smooth singular simplex (~50 LOC) + integration via measure on
  \(\Delta^k\) (~150 LOC) + Stokes for one simplex (~250 LOC) +
  smooth approximation (~200 LOC).
\item \emph{Phase 3 (cohomology comparison map).}  R4.3.1--R4.3.3.
  Routine assembly once Phases 1+2 are in hand; ~250 LOC.
\item \emph{Phase 4 (good-cover argument).}  R4.4.1--R4.4.4.
  Mayer--Vietoris is doable; the existence of good covers (R4.4.3)
  needs Riemannian-manifold infrastructure not in Mathlib.  ~600 LOC.
\end{itemize}

\subsubsection*{Recursive sub-gaps surfaced}

\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{R4-sub-A} (bundled \(\Omega^k(M)\) as a
  \(C^\infty(M)\)-module, R4.1).  ~400 LOC; foundational for
  R5/R6/R7 also.
\item \textbf{R4-sub-B} (smooth singular chains and Stokes on a
  smooth \(k\)-simplex, R4.2.3).  ~300 LOC building on Mathlib's
  measure-theoretic Stokes for boxes.
\item \textbf{R4-sub-C} (good-cover existence on a smooth manifold,
  R4.4.3).  ~250 LOC plus prerequisite Riemannian-metric package
  (~500 LOC of foundational work --- see R5).
\end{itemize}

\begin{lemma}[De Rham (assembly form): via good cover + Mayer--Vietoris]
\label{lem:deRham-overview-stepwise}
\uses{lem:deRham-integration-descends,lem:deRham-iso-via-good-cover}
The Lean target \code{Jacobian.Analysis.DeRham.deRham_overview}
stubs the headline; sub-leaves R4.1.1--R4.4.4 are tracked in
\code{Jacobian.Analysis.DeRham.Overview} and the bottom-up sketch
in \code{Jacobian/StageB/DeRhamComparison.lean}.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.DeRham.deRham_overview}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\paragraph{Lean build status (R4).}
As of this writing, \code{lake build Jacobian.Analysis.DeRham} is
\emph{100\% \texttt{sorry}-free}: every declaration in
\code{Jacobian/Analysis/DeRham/Overview.lean} (the headline
\code{deRham\_overview}, all 15 sub-leaf stubs R4.1.1--R4.4.4, the
three R4-sub-{A,B,C} promotion stubs, and the depth-first
\code{dis}-chain refinements R4.dis-r6--R4.dis-r15 from the rounds
below) closes against the StageB placeholder types.  No transitive
\code{sorry} is pulled in either: the dependency on
\code{Jacobian.StageA.PrismOperator} (and through it on
\code{Jacobian.Periods.\{TietzeReduction, Polygon4gCellular,
SingularH1Homotopy\}}) was severed by extracting
\code{stdSimplex} and \code{SingularSimplex} into a slim core module
\code{Jacobian/StageA/SingularSimplexCore.lean}, which is now the
import shared between \code{StageB/DeRhamComparison.lean} and the
prism-operator file.  All five formerly transitive

\subsubsection*{Stepwise refinement of the proof}

Three rounds of top-down refinement decompose the assembly
lemma above into named sub-leaves.  Each leaf carries a Lean
declaration (or a Mathlib reference) and is marked
\textsf{leanok} when the Lean stub exists, \textsf{notready}
when the statement is still a forward declaration, or
\textsf{mathlibok} when it reduces to a Mathlib theorem.

\begin{lemma}[De Rham step 1: integration descends to cohomology]
\label{lem:deRham-integration-descends}
\uses{input:bundled-omega-k,lem:deRham-integration-simplex,lem:deRham-stokes-simplex,lem:deRham-smooth-quasi-iso}
Integration of forms over smooth singular chains gives a $\C$-linear map $H^{k}_{\mathrm{dR}}(M, \C) \to H^{k}_{\mathrm{sing}}(M, \C)$.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.DeRham.deRham_integration_descends}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Integration is well-defined on smooth simplices]
\label{lem:deRham-integration-simplex}
\uses{lem:dis-r1}
Integration $\int_{\sigma} \omega$ is $\R$-linear in $\omega$ for every smooth singular simplex $\sigma$.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.DeRham.deRham_integration_simplex}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Stokes for a simplex: $\int_{\sigma} d\omega = \int_{\partial\sigma} \omega$]
\label{lem:deRham-stokes-simplex}
\uses{lem:dss-r1}
Stokes' theorem on a smooth singular simplex.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.DeRham.deRham_stokes_simplex}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Smooth-singular into singular is a quasi-isomorphism]
\label{lem:deRham-smooth-quasi-iso}
\uses{lem:dsq-r1}
The inclusion of smooth singular chains into singular chains is a chain-level quasi-isomorphism on a smooth manifold.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.DeRham.deRham_smooth_singular_quasi_iso}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[De Rham step 2: comparison is iso via good covers]
\label{lem:deRham-iso-via-good-cover}
\uses{lem:deRham-mayer-vietoris,lem:deRham-good-cover-exists,lem:deRham-mayer-vietoris,lem:deRham-good-cover-exists,lem:deRham-five-lemma-induction}
On a smooth manifold admitting a good cover, the integration map is an isomorphism on every degree.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.DeRham.deRham_five_lemma_induction}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Mayer--Vietoris exact sequence (both theories)]
\label{lem:deRham-mayer-vietoris}
\uses{lem:dmv-r1}
Both $H^{*}_{\mathrm{dR}}$ and $H^{*}_{\mathrm{sing}}$ satisfy the Mayer--Vietoris exact sequence under an open cover $M = U \cup V$.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.DeRham.deRham_mayer_vietoris}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Existence of a good cover]
\label{lem:deRham-good-cover-exists}
\uses{lem:gce-r1-convex-normal-cover}
Every smooth manifold admits a good cover (every finite intersection contractible), constructed from a Riemannian metric and convex normal-coordinate balls.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.DeRham.deRham_good_cover_exists}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Five-lemma induction over a finite good cover]
\label{lem:deRham-five-lemma-induction}
\uses{lem:dfl-r1}
Over a finite good cover the integration map upgrades from per-chart isomorphisms to a global iso via Mayer--Vietoris and the five lemma.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.DeRham.deRham_five_lemma_induction}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{layman}
There are two ways to count holes in a smooth manifold.  The
\emph{differential-forms} way: a closed form is one with
\(d\omega = 0\); an exact form is one of the shape \(\omega = d\alpha\);
the quotient (closed mod exact) is the de Rham cohomology.  The
\emph{singular-chains} way: a cycle is a formal combination of
simplices with \(\partial = 0\); a boundary is one of the shape
\(\partial \beta\); the dual quotient is the singular cohomology.
De Rham's theorem says these two pictures agree, and the
isomorphism is integration: pair a closed form against a cycle and
read off a number.

Why bother?  The de Rham side is the natural language of analysis on
manifolds (everything is a form, integration is automatic).  The
singular side is the natural language of topology (cycles are loops,
boundaries are filled-in regions).  De Rham unites them.  Without
de Rham, there's no way to compare the analytic genus
(``how many holomorphic 1-forms?'') with the topological genus
(``how many independent loops?'').

The Lean version of this proof is large, partly because Mathlib has
no bundled differential-form package at all --- the
\(\Omega^k\)-module of smooth \(k\)-forms with \(d\), \(\wedge\),
pullback all simultaneously linear and natural, has to be built from
scratch (R4-sub-A).  The good-cover argument also pulls in
Riemannian-metric infrastructure (R4-sub-C, ~500 LOC of
prerequisite).  Total estimated for R4 + supporting sub-gaps:
2500--3500 LOC.

In the meantime the R4 \emph{scaffold} --- the
top-down statement bank with all 15 sub-leaves and the
depth-first refinement of the integration-on-a-simplex chain --- has been
arranged so that \code{lake build Jacobian.Analysis.DeRham} compiles
without any \code{sorry} (transitive or otherwise).  All the real
classical content still has to be filled in; the placeholder types
(\code{Omega := PUnit}, \code{singularHC := PUnit}) make every
intermediate stub trivial today, and replacing them is what
2500--3500 LOC of future Lean will be spent on.
\end{layman}

%======================================================================
\subsection{R5 --- Hodge decomposition on a compact K\"ahler manifold}
\label{subsec:gap-R5-hodge}

The headline (Theorem~\ref{input:hodge-decomposition}) says: on a
compact K\"ahler manifold (in particular every compact Riemann surface
with any Hermitian metric), the Laplace--Beltrami operator
\(\Delta = d\delta + \delta d\) has finite-dimensional kernel, every
de Rham cohomology class has a unique harmonic representative, and on
a K\"ahler manifold harmonic forms split by \((p,q)\)-bidegree.

\subsubsection*{Classical proof}

This is the deepest of the eight gaps and the proof has the most
moving pieces:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \emph{Riemannian metric and Hodge \(*\).}  Pick a
  Riemannian metric \(g\); it induces a metric on cotangent space and
  hence on \(\Lambda^k T^* M\), plus a volume form.  The Hodge star
  \(* : \Omega^k \to \Omega^{n-k}\) is the fibrewise duality.
\item \emph{Codifferential and Laplacian.}  Define \(\delta = (-1)^* * d *\)
  (formal \(L^2\)-adjoint of \(d\)) and \(\Delta = d\delta + \delta d\).
  \(\Delta\) is symmetric, non-negative, commutes with \(d\),
  \(\delta\), \(*\).
\item \emph{Ellipticity + elliptic regularity.}  \(\Delta\) is an
  elliptic second-order differential operator.  Its principal
  symbol is \(|\xi|^2 \cdot \mathrm{id}\), invertible for
  \(\xi \neq 0\).  Elliptic regularity: every distributional
  solution of \(\Delta \omega = f\) with smooth \(f\) is itself
  smooth.  Combined with Rellich--Kondrachov compactness on a
  compact manifold, this makes \(\Delta : H^s_{k+2} \to H^s_k\) (in
  Sobolev norms on forms) Fredholm.
\item \emph{Harmonic decomposition.}  The Fredholm property gives
  \(\Omega^k(M) = \mathcal{H}^k(M) \oplus d(\Omega^{k-1}) \oplus \delta(\Omega^{k+1})\),
  an \(L^2\)-orthogonal decomposition, with
  \(\mathcal{H}^k = \ker \Delta\) the (finite-dimensional) harmonic
  forms.  Every de Rham class has a unique harmonic representative.
\item \emph{K\"ahler bigrading.}  On a K\"ahler manifold, the metric,
  complex structure, and K\"ahler form are mutually compatible.  The
  K\"ahler identities \([\Lambda, \partial] = i\bar\partial^*\) (and
  cousins) imply \(\Delta_d = 2\Delta_{\bar\partial} = 2\Delta_\partial\).
  Hence \(\Delta\) preserves \((p,q)\)-bidegree, the harmonic
  decomposition splits as
  \(\mathcal{H}^k = \bigoplus_{p+q=k} \mathcal{H}^{p,q}\), and Hodge
  symmetry \(\mathcal{H}^{p,q} = \overline{\mathcal{H}^{q,p}}\)
  follows from complex conjugation.
\end{enumerate}

\subsubsection*{Lean proof plan}

Decomposed into 19 sub-leaves under
\code{Jacobian.Analysis.HodgeDecomposition.Overview}, in six phases.
The largest single piece is the Sobolev-elliptic-regularity package
(R5-sub-B); without that, the entire decomposition argument has no
foundation.

\begin{itemize}
\item \emph{Phase 1 (Riemannian + Hodge \(*\)).}  R5.1.1--R5.1.4.
  Needs Riemannian-metric infrastructure (R5-sub-A).  ~400 LOC.
\item \emph{Phase 2 (codifferential + Laplacian).}  R5.2.1--R5.2.3.
  ~200 LOC once Phase 1 lands.
\item \emph{Phase 3 (ellipticity + regularity).}  R5.3.1--R5.3.4.
  Mathlib has neither distributional Sobolev spaces on a manifold,
  nor elliptic regularity, nor Rellich--Kondrachov for forms.
  Estimated 1500--2500 LOC for the package; the largest single
  obstacle to formalizing R5.
\item \emph{Phase 4 (harmonic decomposition).}  R5.4.1--R5.4.4.
  Routine functional analysis once Phase 3 is in hand; ~300 LOC.
\item \emph{Phase 5 (K\"ahler bigrading).}  R5.5.1--R5.5.5.  Mostly
  algebraic; ~400 LOC.
\item \emph{Phase 6 (Riemann surface specialisation).}  R5.6.1,
  R5.6.2.  ~150 LOC.
\end{itemize}

\subsubsection*{Recursive sub-gaps surfaced}

\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{R5-sub-A} (Riemannian metric on a manifold).
  Mathlib has none; needs bundle structure on \(T^*M\).  ~500 LOC,
  shared with R4-sub-C.
\item \textbf{R5-sub-B} (Sobolev spaces of forms + elliptic regularity
  for second-order operators).  The single largest sub-gap in the
  entire eight-node tree; estimated 2500--4000 LOC.  Mathlib has
  Sobolev spaces on \(\R^n\) (\code{Mathlib.Analysis.Distribution})
  but not on manifolds, and not for vector-bundle-valued forms.
\item \textbf{R5-sub-C} (Rellich--Kondrachov compact embedding for
  forms).  ~400 LOC after R5-sub-B.
\item \textbf{R5-sub-D} (Fredholm-alternative on a compact manifold).
  ~300 LOC after R5-sub-B + R5-sub-C.
\item \textbf{R5-sub-E} (K\"ahler-identity package).  ~400 LOC of
  algebraic-geometry-flavored pencils; needs Phase 5 sub-leaves
  R5.5.1--R5.5.5.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lemma}[Hodge decomposition (assembly form): via Fredholm Laplacian]
\label{lem:hodge-decomposition-overview-stepwise}
\uses{lem:hodge-laplacian-kernel-finite-dim,lem:hodge-deRham-iso-harmonic}
The Lean target
\code{Jacobian.Analysis.HodgeDecomposition.hodge_decomposition_overview}
stubs the headline; sub-leaves R5.1.1--R5.6.2 are tracked in
\code{Jacobian.Analysis.HodgeDecomposition.Overview}.

\textbf{Status (post round 11).}  The algebraic finite-dim core of
this assembly is now a real Mathlib-backed theorem: see Round 11
below (\code{Jacobian/Analysis/HodgeDecomposition/AbstractHodgeComplex.lean}).
It proves $V_{1} = \ker\Delta\oplus\mathrm{range}\,d_{0}\oplus\mathrm{range}\,d_{1}^{*}$
for any graded inner-product complex $V_{0}\xrightarrow{d_{0}}V_{1}\xrightarrow{d_{1}}V_{2}$
over $\R$ with $d^{2}=0$.  The remaining gap to the analytic R5 is
elliptic regularity (R5-sub-B), which reduces $L^{2}\Omega^{k}$ to a
finite-dim space; once that lands, the present file is dispatched
end-to-end.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.HodgeDecomposition.hodge_decomposition_overview,hodge_decomposition}
\leanok
\end{lemma}


\subsubsection*{Stepwise refinement of the proof}

Three rounds of top-down refinement decompose the assembly
lemma above into named sub-leaves.  Each leaf carries a Lean
declaration (or a Mathlib reference) and is marked
\textsf{leanok} when the Lean stub exists, \textsf{notready}
when the statement is still a forward declaration, or
\textsf{mathlibok} when it reduces to a Mathlib theorem.

\begin{lemma}[Hodge step 1: harmonic kernel is finite-dimensional]
\label{lem:hodge-laplacian-kernel-finite-dim}
\uses{input:sobolev-elliptic-regularity,lem:hodge-laplacian-elliptic,lem:hodge-elliptic-regularity,lem:hodge-laplacian-fredholm}
For a compact oriented Riemannian manifold $(M, g)$, the kernel of the Laplace--Beltrami operator on $\Omega^{k}(M)$ is finite-dimensional.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.HodgeDecomposition.hodge_laplacian_kernel_finite_dim}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Laplacian is elliptic]
\label{lem:hodge-laplacian-elliptic}
\uses{lem:hle-r1}
The principal symbol of $\Delta = d\delta + \delta d$ is $|\xi|^{2} \cdot \mathrm{id}$, hence invertible for $\xi \neq 0$.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.HodgeDecomposition.hodge_laplacian_elliptic}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Elliptic regularity (Sobolev)]
\label{lem:hodge-elliptic-regularity}
\uses{input:sobolev-elliptic-regularity}
Smooth solutions of $\Delta\omega = f$ for smooth $f$ are smooth; the kernel of $\Delta$ over smooth forms equals the kernel over distributions.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.HodgeDecomposition.hodge_elliptic_regularity}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Fredholm property of $\Delta$]
\label{lem:hodge-laplacian-fredholm}
\uses{input:sobolev-elliptic-regularity}
Combined ellipticity + Rellich--Kondrachov makes $\Delta : H^{s+2}(\Omega^{k}) \to H^{s}(\Omega^{k})$ Fredholm.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.HodgeDecomposition.hodge_laplacian_fredholm}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Hodge step 2: de Rham--harmonic isomorphism]
\label{lem:hodge-deRham-iso-harmonic}
\uses{lem:hodge-orthogonal-decomposition,lem:hodge-unique-harmonic-representative,lem:hodge-bigrading-split}
Each de Rham cohomology class on a compact Riemannian manifold has a unique harmonic representative.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.HodgeDecomposition.hodge_deRham_iso_harmonic}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Hodge $L^{2}$-orthogonal decomposition]
\label{lem:hodge-orthogonal-decomposition}
\uses{lem:hod-r1,lem:hod-r49,lem:hod-r50,lem:hod-r51}
$\Omega^{k}(M) = \mathcal{H}^{k}(M) \oplus d(\Omega^{k-1}) \oplus \delta(\Omega^{k+1})$, $L^{2}$-orthogonally.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.HodgeDecomposition.hodge_orthogonal_decomposition}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Each de Rham class has a harmonic representative]
\label{lem:hodge-unique-harmonic-representative}
\uses{lem:huh-r1}
The projection $\Omega^{k} \to \mathcal{H}^{k}$ descends to an iso $H^{k}_{\mathrm{dR}}(M) \cong \mathcal{H}^{k}(M)$.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.HodgeDecomposition.hodge_unique_harmonic_representative}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[K\"ahler bigrading of harmonic forms]
\label{lem:hodge-bigrading-split}
\uses{lem:hbs-r1}
On a K\"ahler manifold the Laplacians for $d, \partial, \bar\partial$ are proportional, so $\mathcal{H}^{k} = \bigoplus_{p+q=k} \mathcal{H}^{p,q}$ with Hodge symmetry $\mathcal{H}^{p,q} \cong \overline{\mathcal{H}^{q,p}}$.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.HodgeDecomposition.hodge_bigrading_split}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{layman}
The Hodge decomposition theorem is the most analytic of the eight
gaps and the one that pulls in the largest piece of missing
foundational work in Mathlib.  The headline statement is:
on a compact K\"ahler manifold (a Riemann surface with any choice of
Hermitian metric counts) every cohomology class has a \emph{unique}
harmonic representative --- a form lying in the kernel of the
Laplace--Beltrami operator \(\Delta = d\delta + \delta d\).  On a
compact Riemann surface, harmonic 1-forms further split by type:
holomorphic 1-forms (\((1,0)\)-type) and antiholomorphic 1-forms
(\((0,1)\)-type), each \(g\)-dimensional, summing to \(2g\).

The proof's deepest layer is elliptic-PDE machinery: ellipticity of
\(\Delta\) makes it (modulo a finite-dimensional null space) an
isomorphism on appropriately defined Sobolev spaces of forms.
That's what gives unique harmonic representatives.  The K\"ahler
identities then sharpen the picture by revealing that the Laplacian
respects the \((p,q)\)-bidegree decomposition, so harmonic forms
themselves split by type.

For Lean, the sticking point is that Mathlib v4.28.0 has no Sobolev
spaces of forms on a manifold and no elliptic regularity for
manifold-level operators.  Building this from scratch is the
single largest task in the eight-gap tree (R5-sub-B alone:
2500--4000 LOC).  Once that's in hand, the rest of the decomposition
(harmonic representatives, K\"ahler bigrading, Hodge symmetry) is
routine modern functional analysis.  Total estimated for R5 with all
sub-gaps: 5500--8500 LOC.
\end{layman}

%======================================================================
\subsection{R6 --- Hodge / de Rham rank for a Riemann surface}
\label{subsec:gap-R6-hodge-deRham}

The headline (Theorem~\ref{input:hodge-deRham}) says: for a compact
connected oriented Riemann surface \(X\) of analytic genus \(g\),
\(\mathrm{rank}_{\Z}\,H_1(X, \Z) = 2g\).  This is the assembly
gap that consumes R4 (de Rham), R5 (Hodge decomposition), and R7
(Dolbeault) and outputs the integer rank used by the
period-lattice fullness argument.

\subsubsection*{Classical proof}

A three-step assembly:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \emph{Hodge \(\Rightarrow\)
  \(\dim_{\C} H^1_{\mathrm{dR}}(X, \C) = 2g\).}  Apply R5 to split
  \(H^1_{\mathrm{dR}}(X, \C) = H^{1,0}(X) \oplus H^{0,1}(X)\).
  Apply R7 to identify \(H^{1,0}(X) \cong H^0(X, \Omega^1_X)\), which
  by definition has dimension \(g\).  Apply Hodge symmetry
  (R5.5.5) to get \(\dim H^{0,1} = g\).
\item \emph{De Rham \(\Rightarrow\)
  \(H^1_{\mathrm{sing}}(X, \R) \cong H^1_{\mathrm{dR}}(X, \R)\).}
  Apply R4.  Real dimension is \(2g\).
\item \emph{Universal coefficients \(\Rightarrow\)
  \(\mathrm{rank}_{\Z} H_1(X, \Z) = \dim_\R H^1_{\mathrm{sing}}(X, \R)\).}
  In degree 1 on a compact CW manifold, the universal-coefficient
  theorem gives \(H^1(X, \R) \cong \mathrm{Hom}(H_1(X, \Z), \R)\),
  whose real dimension equals the integer rank of \(H_1(X, \Z)\).
\end{enumerate}

\subsubsection*{Lean proof plan}

Decomposed into 11 sub-leaves under
\code{Jacobian.Analysis.HodgeDeRham.Overview}, in four phases:

\begin{itemize}
\item \emph{Phase 1 (real dim of \(H^1_{\mathrm{dR}}\)).}
  R6.1.1--R6.1.5.  All routine assembly above R5/R7; ~200 LOC.
\item \emph{Phase 2 (de Rham comparison).}  R6.2.1, R6.2.2.  Pure
  invocation of R4; ~50 LOC.
\item \emph{Phase 3 (singular-to-integer-homology).}  R6.3.1--R6.3.4.
  Universal coefficients and rank-Hom lemmas; Mathlib has the algebra
  but not the singular variant for manifolds.  ~400 LOC.
\item \emph{Phase 4 (final).}  R6.4.1.  ~30 LOC.
\end{itemize}

R6 is small in itself (estimated ~700 LOC) but it depends on three of
the eight major gaps simultaneously; it cannot be discharged in
isolation.

\subsubsection*{Recursive sub-gaps surfaced}

\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{R6-sub-A} (universal-coefficient theorem in degree 1
  for a CW manifold, R6.3.1).  Algebraic UCT exists in Mathlib
  (\code{Mathlib.Algebra.Homology.UniversalCoefficient});
  geometric variant for \(H^*_{\mathrm{sing}}(X, \Z)\) does not.
  ~250 LOC.
\item \textbf{R6-sub-B} (\(H_1(X, \Z)\) finitely generated for a
  compact CW manifold, R6.3.2).  Mathlib has \(H_*\) for chain
  complexes but not the manifold-level finite-CW theorem.
  ~150 LOC, after a triangulation is in hand (so this depends on R1).
\item \textbf{R6-sub-C} (\(\dim_\R \mathrm{Hom}(A, \R) = \mathrm{rank}_\Z A\)
  for a fg abelian group, R6.3.3).  Routine; ~80 LOC; Mathlib has
  the building blocks.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lemma}[Hodge / de Rham rank (assembly form): via classical chain]
\label{lem:hodge-deRham-overview-stepwise}
\uses{lem:hodge-deRham-dim-h1-dR,lem:hodge-deRham-rank-eq-dim}
The Lean target \code{Jacobian.Analysis.HodgeDeRham.hodge_deRham_overview}
stubs the headline; sub-leaves R6.1.1--R6.4.1 are tracked in
\code{Jacobian.Analysis.HodgeDeRham.Overview}.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.HodgeDeRham.hodge_deRham_overview}
\leanok
\end{lemma}


\subsubsection*{Stepwise refinement of the proof}

Three rounds of top-down refinement decompose the assembly
lemma above into named sub-leaves.  Each leaf carries a Lean
declaration (or a Mathlib reference) and is marked
\textsf{leanok} when the Lean stub exists, \textsf{notready}
when the statement is still a forward declaration, or
\textsf{mathlibok} when it reduces to a Mathlib theorem.

\begin{lemma}[Hodge / de Rham step 1: $\dim_{\C} H^{1}_{\mathrm{dR}}(X) = 2g$]
\label{lem:hodge-deRham-dim-h1-dR}
\uses{input:hodge-decomposition,input:dolbeault}
Apply Hodge decomposition + Dolbeault: $H^{1}_{\mathrm{dR}}(X, \C) = H^{1,0}(X) \oplus H^{0,1}(X)$, both summands of dimension $g$.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.HodgeDeRham.hodge_deRham_dim_h1_dR}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Hodge / de Rham step 2: rank-via-dim universal coefficients]
\label{lem:hodge-deRham-rank-eq-dim}
\uses{input:de-rham-theorem,lem:hodge-deRham-uct-degree-one,lem:hodge-deRham-h1-fg,lem:hodge-deRham-hom-rank}
Apply de Rham + UCT: $\mathrm{rank}_{\Z}\,H_{1}(X, \Z) = \dim_{\C}\,H^{1}_{\mathrm{dR}}(X)$.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.HodgeDeRham.hodge_deRham_rank_eq_dim}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Universal coefficients in degree 1 for a CW manifold]
\label{lem:hodge-deRham-uct-degree-one}
\uses{lem:hdu-r1}
$H^{1}(X, \R) \cong \mathrm{Hom}(H_{1}(X, \Z), \R)$ on a compact CW manifold.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.HodgeDeRham.hodge_deRham_uct_degree_one}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[$H_{1}(X, \Z)$ is finitely generated]
\label{lem:hodge-deRham-h1-fg}
\uses{lem:hdf-r1}
$H_{1}(X, \Z)$ is finitely generated for a compact CW manifold (built from a triangulation, R1).
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.HodgeDeRham.hodge_deRham_h1_fg}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Rank-Hom: $\dim_{\R}\,\mathrm{Hom}(A, \R) = \mathrm{rank}_{\Z}\,A$ for $A$ fg]
\label{lem:hodge-deRham-hom-rank}
\uses{lem:hdr-r1}
Standard linear algebra over $\Z$: real-Hom dimension equals integer rank for a finitely-generated abelian group.  See Chain G2 (passes \ref{lem:hdr-r1}--\ref{lem:hdr-r5}, then \ref{lem:hdr-r6}--\ref{lem:hdr-r10}, then \ref{lem:hdr-r11}--\ref{lem:hdr-r15}) for the full 15-pass refinement into Mathlib endpoints; the assembly is sorry-free in Lean.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.HodgeDeRham.hodge_deRham_hom_dim_eq_rank}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Hodge / de Rham: real de Rham bridge to singular cohomology]
\label{lem:hodge-deRham-deRham-bridge}
\uses{input:de-rham-theorem}
$\dim_\R H^1_{\mathrm{dR}}(X, \R) = \dim_\R H^1_{\mathrm{sing}}(X, \R)$ for a compact connected smooth manifold.  This is the de Rham theorem in degree 1: integration over smooth singular chains gives a quasi-isomorphism on cochain complexes, and on degree-1 cohomology this restricts to an $\R$-linear isomorphism.  Mathlib v4.28.0 lacks the de Rham complex on manifolds, so this leaf is a frontier obligation; see \code{deRhamComparisonMap1\_isLinearEquiv}, refined into \emph{Stokes} (\code{deRhamComparisonMap1\_vanishes\_on\_exact}), \emph{surjectivity} (\code{deRhamComparisonMap1\_surjective}), and \emph{injectivity} (\code{deRhamComparisonMap1\_kernel\_subset\_exact}).
\lean{JacobianChallenge.HolomorphicForms.realDim_deRhamH1_eq_realDim_singularH1}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Hodge / de Rham: UCT bridge to $\mathrm{Hom}_\Z(H_1, \R)$]
\label{lem:hodge-deRham-uct-bridge}
\uses{lem:hodge-deRham-uct-degree-one}
$\dim_\R H^1_{\mathrm{sing}}(X, \R) = \dim_\R \mathrm{Hom}_\Z(H_1(X, \Z), \R)$.  Universal coefficients with $\R$ coefficients: the short exact sequence
\[
0 \to \mathrm{Ext}^1_\Z(H_0(X, \Z), \R) \to H^1_{\mathrm{sing}}(X, \R) \to \mathrm{Hom}_\Z(H_1(X, \Z), \R) \to 0
\]
collapses to an isomorphism because $\R$ is divisible (hence injective as a $\Z$-module), so $\mathrm{Ext}^1_\Z(\,\cdot\, \R) = 0$.  Lean implementation: \code{realDimSingularH1\_eq\_finrank\_intHom1} (currently a definitional unfold on the chosen surrogate model of $H^1_{\mathrm{sing}}$).
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Periods.realDimSingularH1_eq_finrank_intHom1}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Hodge / de Rham: real-of-complex equality for $H^1_{\mathrm{dR}}$]
\label{lem:hodge-deRham-real-of-complex}
\uses{input:de-rham-theorem}
$\dim_\R H^1_{\mathrm{dR}}(X, \R) = \dim_\C H^1_{\mathrm{dR}}(X, \C)$ on a compact connected complex manifold.  The two sides are linked by the canonical $\C \otimes_\R \,\cdot\,$ isomorphism between real- and complex-coefficient de Rham cohomology, and complexification doubles the real dimension exactly when ``$\dim_\R$'' on the LHS counts the underlying $\R$-module of an $\R$-vector space and ``$\dim_\C$'' on the RHS counts the complexification's $\C$-dimension.  In our finrank surrogate this is a routine algebraic identity: $\mathrm{finrank}_\R V = \mathrm{finrank}_\C(\C \otimes_\R V)$ for any finite-dimensional $\R$-vector space $V$.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.HolomorphicForms.realDim_deRhamH1_eq_complexDim_deRhamH1ℂ}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\subsubsection*{R6 frontier sorry inventory (Mathlib v4.28.0)}

The full R6 chain assembles, in Lean, into the headline equality
\code{2 * analyticGenus \C\ X = Module.finrank \Z\ (IntegralOneCycle X)}
through the named Lean target
\code{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.HodgeDeRham.hodge\_deRham\_overview}.
The chain is sorry-free \emph{above} the named frontier leaves but
ultimately bottoms out, on Mathlib v4.28.0, at the following six
honest classical inputs (now linked to generated Lean hooks in their respective
chains):

\begin{enumerate}
\item \emph{Cellular--singular comparison, finite generation.}
\code{IntegralOneCycle\_finite\_of\_cellular} in
\code{Jacobian/Periods/CellularHomologyRS.lean}.  Hatcher 2.35 for
$H_1$.  Blueprint chain: G1 (\ref{lem:hdf-r1}--\ref{lem:hdf-r15}),
hub R6-sub-A.  Mathlib status: ABSENT (cellular chain complex on a
topological space is missing, as is its comparison with singular
homology).

\item \emph{Cellular--singular comparison, torsion-freeness.}
\code{IntegralOneCycle\_free\_of\_cellular} in the same file.
\emph{Not} a generic finite-CW fact --- torsion can appear (e.g.\
$H_1(\mathbb{RP}^2) = \mathbb{Z}/2$); the closed-orientable-2-manifold
case follows from the polygonal model
$H_1(\Sigma_g) = \mathbb{Z}^{2g}$ or from Poincar\'e duality.
Mathlib status: ABSENT (polygonal model identification absent;
Poincar\'e duality on manifolds absent).

\item \emph{De Rham theorem, surjectivity.}
\code{deRhamComparisonMap1\_prescribed\_period\_correct} in
\code{Jacobian/HolomorphicForms/DeRhamComparisonMap.lean}.  Given a
$\R$-valued cocycle on $H_1$, build a closed 1-form whose periods
realise it.  Blueprint chain: \ref{subsec:gap-R4-de-rham}, Chain E
(\ref{lem:deRham-integration-simplex}--).  Mathlib status: ABSENT
(no de Rham complex on manifolds; partition-of-unity primitive
construction absent).

\item \emph{De Rham theorem, injectivity.}
\code{deRhamComparisonMap1\_zero\_period\_potential} in the same file.
A closed 1-form integrating to zero on every cycle is exact.  Blueprint
chain: same as item 3.  Mathlib status: ABSENT (sheaf cohomology
comparison on manifolds is partial; global path-integral primitive on
a non-simply-connected manifold absent).

\item \emph{Bundle norm compatibility.}
\code{holomorphicOneForm\_trivialization\_norm\_compat} in
\code{Jacobian/HolomorphicForms/CompactRiemannSurface.lean}.  Local
trivialisations of the cotangent bundle on $X$ preserve the
section-wise norm.  Mathlib status: ABSENT for the holomorphic
cotangent bundle; the smooth-real analogue is partial.

\item \emph{Sup-norm completeness of $\Omega^1_{\mathrm{hol}}(X)$.}
The candidate-tendsto leaf (line 265) plus the Arzel\`a-Ascoli
total-boundedness leaf (line 739) of
\code{Jacobian/HolomorphicForms/CompactRiemannSurface.lean}.
Underlying classical content: Weierstrass (uniform limit of
holomorphic functions is holomorphic) plus Montel (a uniformly bounded
family is sup-norm-precompact).  Both used to show
$\Omega^1_{\mathrm{hol}}(X)$ is finite-dimensional via Riesz
(\code{FiniteDimensional.of\_locallyCompactSpace}).  Mathlib status:
Arzel\`a-Ascoli present at the abstract level; the Montel/Weierstrass
specialisation to bundle-section spaces is absent.
\end{enumerate}

These six obligations are the entire frontier between the current
sorry-free assembly of R6 and a fully closed proof.  None can be
discharged \emph{within} R6 --- each is a named umbrella with its own
chain, and each is one of the eight ``classical analysis gaps''
catalogued in this section (R4, R5/R7, R6-sub-A, R8 (Riesz)).

\begin{layman}
R6 is the simplest of the eight in terms of its own work, but it
sits at the confluence of three other gaps --- it can't be done
without R4, R5, and R7 all in place.  The headline says: on a genus-\(g\)
Riemann surface, the integer first homology group has rank exactly
\(2g\).  In words: there are \(2g\) topologically independent loops
on \(X\), no more, no less.

The proof is a chain of three identifications.  First, Hodge (R5) +
Dolbeault (R7) say that the complex de Rham cohomology has dimension
\(2g\) --- \(g\) holomorphic 1-forms plus \(g\) antiholomorphic ones.
Second, de Rham (R4) says singular cohomology over \(\R\) matches
de Rham cohomology, so the real singular cohomology also has
